Environmental and economic optimals
ii
==PAGE:4==
Abstract
With the climate changing due to human activities, it is of high importance to limit Greenhouse
Gas Emissions (GHGe). Denmark has for example set the goal of reducing GHGe by 70%
before 2030, and become completely climate neutral by 2050. With a significant amount of
these emissions coming from buildings, both the construction and heating, it is of high interest
to reduce these. Because of this, the Danish Building Regulation 2018 (BR18) is being modified
to include limits to GHGe through the use of a required Life Cycle Analysis (LCA) for all buildings,
which will come into effect in mid 2025. Throughout the next years, these limits will be reduced
every second year, reaching the strictest requirements in 2029.
Optimisation between the embodied and operational emissions is therefore an important part
of this puzzle, which BR18 does not help with. Through a literature study a number of gaps
have been found, which include the optimal insulation thickness in Denmark not being known,
a lack of knowledge in how climate change will impact this optimum, whether the economic cost
incentivizes this optimum, what the impact of missing it is, and whether the current requirements
for windows, a common hole in the building envelope, is and will be worth it environmentally.
As such, this paper aims to answer these questions for a Single Family (SF) and Multi Family
(MF) house.
These optimal values for the climate envelope will be investigated through dynamic building
simulations in the program IDA ICE. Future weather files for three scenarios are used to account
for climate change. Average emissions for insulation types are used and modified to reflect the
expected embodied emissions in the future. Operational emissions from heating are included
through two different Emission Factors (EF). One of these is the official projected EF’s from
BR18, calculated and updated by Cowi in 2020, and the other is a projected EF calculated in
2023 by Artelia.
Using this method, the optimal insulation thicknesses for the building envelope have been found
for both the SF and MF houses. These optimums depend heavily on which EF is used, with the
EF calculated by Cowi often resulting in insulation thicknesses above 500mm. When using a
lower EF, these thicknesses range from 300 −350mm for the exterior walls and roofs, with sim­
ilar thicknesses for both buildings. The optimal insulation thicknesses for the terrain deck have
been found to be both lower and a lot more varied, depending on the building type and time pe­
riod. It is further found that climate change may reduce these thicknesses slightly, although the
difference is less than other literature has found. Levelized Cost of Carbon Abatement (LCCA)
values have also been calculated using the equivalent of 25mm Thermal Conductivity (TC)
0.037 mineral wool insulation, with the baseline set using the minimum heat loss requirements
from BR18. From these, it has been found that while using more than this insulation is often
both economically and environmentally preferable, there is a point where the cost increases
exponentially. Where this point is varies based on the time period, the TC of the insulation and
the building part.
The economic optimums have been found to be much lower than the environmental ones. The
last main finding is that missing this found optimum results in a larger increase, in both GHGe
and cost, when using too little insulation rather than too much. These results are based on
initial prices for the public and, as such, are expected to differ significantly for larger construction
companies.
These results can be used to aid decision makers in deciding both between minimum require­
ments in future building regulations and in wider decisions between policies aiming to reduce
GHGe.
Environmental and economic optimals

==PAGE:9==
1
Intro
1.1
Background
The climate of the world is undeniably changing due to human activities, with an increase in the
global surface temperature of 1.1oC when comparing the 2011­2020 period with 1850­1900, and
is more likely than not to reach 1.5oC before 2040 even under a very low emission scenario [1].
To combat this change, global action is required, with Denmark setting goals to reduce Green­
house Gas emissions (GHGe) by 70% in 2030 compared to 1990 and becoming completely
climate neutral in 2050 [2].
Large portions of GHGe stem from the energy sector and the construction sector. In 2010 35%
of GHGe were caused by the energy sector and 6.4% directly from the building sector. However,
a large share of the energy is used to heat and cool buildings. If these indirect emissions are
added to the building sector, then they would be responsible for 19% of the total emissions [3].
As such both the operational energy of buildings is a major contributor to global warming.
More local data for Denmark shows that construction is responsible for 27% of the country’s
impact on climate change [4]. To account for this The Danish Building Regulation 2018 (BR18)
made an addendum valid from 2023. Hence, all new buildings above 1000m2 are required to
do a Life Cycle Analysis (LCA) and must not exceed a threshold of 12kg CO2 −eq./m2/year.
This value is expected to be reduced gradually until 2029 along with an additional requirement
for the building process of 1.5kg CO2 −eq./m2/year[5].
This however, is most likely not enough as, according to Tozan et al, Denmark would have to
reduce its total emissions by 97% to comply with the boundary of 0.3kg CO2 −eq./m2/year.
Currently no buildings comply with this limit [6], and even the lowest thresholds expected in
2029 are much higher than this [5]. Tozan further finds that 91% of future emissions will be
related to embodied emissions peaking in 2041 due to an increase in the predicted amount of
construction [6]. This massive influence of embodied emissions also make sense on a more
global scale for construction, as about 50% of extracted materials are currently used in the built
environment [7].
With the expected increases in the built environment it is therefore important to limit both the
emissions from Resource Consumption (RC) and operation of the building. Historically focus
has been on reducing the operational energy of buildings. While this is still a key area it is also
crucial to mitigate the embodied carbon emissions leading to a shift in focus [6]. An emerging
issue when working on this area is the temporal representativeness. Technological develop­
ments are not included in databases, leading to significant issues when using LCA to guide
industries [4]. The same is true for the energy used to heat and cool the buildings where the
emission factors are expected to be lower in the future than they currently are [8]. Due to this
dynamic nature it is necessary to apply prospective Life Cycle Analysis (pLCA) where future
scenarios can be taken into account [4].
1.2
Objectives
This thesis aims to investigate the optimal insulation thicknesses to limit GHGe over the build­
ings full lifetime. The project will look at both Single Family (SF) house’s and Multi Family (MF)
house’s built in 2025, 2050 and 2100. A key result will be whether the optimal thickness for the
years differs both due to expected changes in the climate and therefore temperature over the
years as well as changes to the Danish energy mix.
 Environmental and economic optimals
1
==PAGE:10==
2
Literature review
2.1
The Danish Building regulation
BR18 contains the rules and regulations that all new and renovated buildings in Denmark must
follow. For this thesis, the most relevant chapter is Chapter 11 ”Energy Consumption and Cli­
mate Impact”, which contains §250 to §298. The crux of the chapter is already mentioned in
§250 which states that ”Buildings must be planned, established, converted and maintained in
order to maintain unnecessary consumption of energy for heating, Domestic Hot Water (DHW),
cooling, ventilation and lighting with due respect of the use of the building and the scope of the
building work.” [9]
The relevant paragraphs have to do with the heating requirements for residential buildings and
limits to the GHGe over their lifetimes. All information in Section 2.1 is from BR18 [9] unless
otherwise specified.
2.1.1
Heating requirements
The paragraphs related to heating occur between §252 and §260. As such the following rules
have to be followed:
§252:
This paragraph states that energy used for heating during the buildings lifetime must weighed
according to the following factors:
1. 1.9 for electricity
2. 0.85 for district heating
3. For other heating sources a factor of 1.0 and the relevant efficiency
§257:
This paragraph deals with the minimum requirements for the building envelope regarding heat
losses. The maximum heat loss coefficients for relevant building components can be seen in
Table 2.1.

The total energy supply demand accounts for heating, ventilation, cooling and DHW.

2.1.2
Emission requirements
BR18 contains two paragraphs regarding LCA, both dealing with emissions from buildings.
§297:
A calculation for all building’s climate impact over its lifecycle must be made. The impact of
the calculation should be in the unit kg CO2 −eq./m2/year and done according to DS/EN
15978:2012. with a lifetime of 50 years. The following parts must be included:
A1: Raw material supply
A2: Transport
A3: Manufacturing
B4: Replacement
B6: Operational energy use
C3: Waste processing

§298:
Currently, there is an additional requirement for buildings with a heated floor area above 1000m2,
where the emissions may not exceed 12.0kg CO2−eq./m2/year. This, along with other require­
ments on GHGe, was included as an addendum to BR18 from the 1st of January 2023. This
addendum is applicable until the 30th of June 2025, where more strict regulations for all build­
ings, regardless of size, take effect. These regulations will gradually become stricter until 2029,
after which no further changes have been announced. The limits can be seen in Table 2.4 but
are not included directly in BR18 at the time of writing.

An additional requirement will be implemented from the 1st of July 2025 where the maximum
emissions for the building process will be limited to 1.5kg CO2−eq./m2/year. This encompasses
transport of materials to and from the construction site, energy and fuel consumption during the
construction and material waste on site. From an LCA perspective this limit covers A4­A5 [5].
As such this is not a part of BR18 at the time of writing either.
2.2
Emission factors
The emissions factors currently used in BR18, as shown in Table 2.3, were originally calculated
by Cowi, an international engineering company based in Denmark [10], in 2016 and updated
in 2020 [8]. The EF for electricity and District Heating (DH) varies greatly depending on the
location, typically at the country level, although variations within a country can easily occur
depending on the grid. An example of a country with a very low EF for electricity is Norway.
This is mainly due to a large ratio of hydropower, responsible for 95% of the country’s electricity.
The Norwegian energy mix has an EF at 0.018 CO2 −eq/kWh [11].
The same paper sets the average European electricity mix at 0.136kg CO2 −eq/kWh, which
according to BR18 fits pretty well with the Danish mix in 2025 [9]. As mentioned, the EF’s used
in BR18 were calculated by Cowi and updated in 2020. Therefore these EF’s will be referred to
as being Cowi’s EF for the rest of the thesis.
While these EF’s are much higher than Norway’s, major reductions are planned via projections
of the energy grid. By 2040 the expected EF for the Danish electricity mix is 0.0403 CO2 −
eq/kWh [8]. According to an earlier study by Gaarder et al, see Section 2.4.2 for more details, an
EF above 0.025−0.030kg CO2 −eq/kWh resulted in GHGe being dominated by the operational
energy in all cases.
The EF’s from Cowi does not quite reach this value, suggesting that the insulation thicknesses
required to optimise the emissions would not be reasonable. Cowi however, is not the only
company who has produced future EF in a Danish context. In 2023 Artelia, a major Danish
consulting engineering firm [12], produced expected EF’s for electricity, DH and pipe gas [13]
which are shown in Table 2.5.
Energy source

5
==PAGE:14==
2.2. Emission factors
The calculated EF’s from Artelia are consistently lower than Cowi’s, and quickly reach below the
0.025 −0.030kg CO2 −eq/kWh found by Gaarder. As such the optimal insulation thicknesses
may not be unreasonable if these EF’s are closer to the actual EF’s in the future. The yearly
EF’s for electricity, DH and pipe gas from Cowi and Artelia can be seen in Figure 2.1. Due to
these differences a closer look into the assumptions behind the calculations has been made in
the following section.
Figure 2.1: Emission factors from Cowi and Artelia
2.2.1
Basis for Cowi EF calculation
The official EF’s made by Cowi were updated in 2020 based on a projection of energy sources
called ”Danmarks Energifremskrivning 2018”. This projection includes Electricity and DH from
2020 to 2040 with changes every 5 years. The projection is building on existing technologies
with no changes to efficiency and the intended use of the calculated EF is to compare the lifetime
emissions of buildings in a Danish context. All information in this section is from Cowi’s updated
emission factors from 2020 [8], unless otherwise specified.
Some general assumptions made by Cowi include:
Historic LCA data have been used, including the database ”GaBi Professional version 6”.
Other databases have also been used, e.g. the EASEWASTE database which was used
for all trash. Other databases are not mentioned by name and it is unclear whether more
have been used or not.
If the data available did not fit the Danish context it was modified to fit better.
EFs are found every 5 years from 2020 to 2040. After the year 2040 the EF is assumed
to be constant.
Emissions are limited to production within Denmark and does not include import or export.
It is stated that the EF produced as such cannot be compared to other factors where this
is included. For the case of this thesis a comparison will still be made with this difference
as a major factor.
Environmental and economic optimals
6
==PAGE:15==
2.2. Emission factors
Figure 2.2: Projected energy sources for electricity used by Cowi [8]
The projection Cowi uses (”Danmarks Energifremskrivning 2018”) for electricity generation is
dominated by wind energy, followed by solar and then co­generation power plants which pro­
duce both electricity and heat using various fuels. Initially a small portion is produced through
fossil fuels in condensation plants which only produce electricity. The use of condensation
plants is projected to diminish to nearly zero by 2030.
Cowi further includes a 7% loss on the consumption side, along with a number of assumptions
for each source which will be presented in a short list.
Solar: Electricity produced from the major types of Photovoltaics (PV) installations is in­
cluded. To attribute the emissions pr. kWh, the efficiency for each type is taken into
consideration along with the solar irradiance in Denmark. Production is included and a
lifetime of 20 years is used.
Wind: Wind energy is produced through both land and ocean based wind turbines. While
the emissions and efficiencies between these vary, it is not specified how much of each is
included. A lifetime of 20 years is used for both types.
Industry: Industrial heat emissions are attributed to the process and the heat is as such
treated as a waste product and therefore carbon neutral.
Co­generation power plants:
Trash: An average emission from incineration of trash in a co­generation power plant
is used. The total emissions depend greatly on the composition of the trash during
incineration. No data exists for this in the future and as such historical values for
household trash are assumed to stay constant. Transport to the plant is not included
in the emissions and it is specified that 42.4% of emissions from trash incineration is
attributed to electricity generation.
Biogas: The average Danish composition of organic material used for biogasification
is used and assumed constant.
Biomass: Included through the GaBi database without modification
Environmental and economic optimals
7
==PAGE:16==
2.2. Emission factors
Fossil fuels: Fuel oil, gas oil and coal are included as fossil fuels. Natural gas is
assumed treated similarly but not mentioned.
Condensation plants:
Biogas: The average Danish composition of organic material used for bio gasification
is used and assumed constant for all years.
Biomass: Included through the GaBi database without modification
Fossil fuels: Fuel oil, gas oil and coal are included as fossil fuels without modification.
Figure 2.3: Projected energy sources for DH used by Cowi [8]
According to the projection Cowi uses, most of the DH is produced through co­generation power
plants followed by boilers. The ratios remain mostly stable throughout the years, which can be
seen in the EF’s found for DH as shown in Table 2.3 and Figure 2.1. While the EF does decrease,
it is not to the same degree as electricity. A large portion of the DH EF decrease can probably be
attributed to a slight electrification from heat pumps and the lowered emissions from electricity.
Cowi includes a 20% energy loss along with a number of assumptions for each source, which
follows:
Solar: Solar heat is based on flat PV with a yearly efficiency of 450kWh/m2 and a lifetime
of 20 years.
Geothermal: A conservative estimation of efficiency is used with a production of 10 times
the energy used in geothermal plants. Efficiencies are assumed constant and plants are
not included in emissions.
Electric immersion heater: Emissions from the electricity use is included and allocated to
the heat generation.
Heat pumps (Excess heat): Heat pumps driven by excess heat are assumed to have a
COP of 5.0. The heat used is treated as a waste product and therefore carbon neutral.
Large scale heat pumps with a lifetime of 20 years is used, and the maximal effect is used
during the heating season from mid September to mid May. It is not clear whether this is

At the end of the document Cowi includes some recommendations for future work. These
include making a projection for the trash composition during the time frame for further precision,
including specific GHGe for the types of biomass used, having a third party verification and
updating the EF’s at least every 5 years.
2.2.2
Basis for Artelia EF calculation
Artelia based their calculations on a projection from The Danish Energy Agency. This projection
accounts for the expected changes in energy sources for electricity, DH and pipe gas from
2022 to 2050 in 5 year intervals. This projection takes efficiency and losses for technologies
into account, as well as political goals, e.g. a 70% reduction in GHGe by 2030 compared to
1990[14]. As there is no available data from 2050­2075, Artelia has calculated that period as
static. They used data from Analyseforudsætninger til Energinet 2022 (AF22) [14], analysis
assumptions for the energy net 2022, supplemented by the ’LCA for Experts’ dataset. Artelia’s
calculations do include the import and export of electricity. All assumptions in this section are
from Artelia’s main document for EF calculation [13] unless otherwise specified.

2.2.3
Comparison of EF calculations
Cowi’s and Artelia’s calculations include a number of different assumptions, most of which are
based on differences in the projections used. Other relevant differences include the difference
in treatment of biological materials and waste, and the inclusion of import and export. As men­
tioned Cowi includes some recommendations for future work at the end of their updated report.
These recommendations include [8]:
A projection of future trash composition
Specific Global Warming Potential (GWP)’s for different types of biomass
Having a third party verification of the calculations
Updating the calculations at least every 5 years
Artelia does include some of these recommendations in their calculations.
Projection of future trash composition: Artelia does not include projection of trash composition
in their calculations. Trash is instead treated as a waste product and therefore carbon neutral
[13].
Differentiating the types of biomass: Artelia does differentiate between different types of biomass
as seen in the presentation of their assumptions. In practice, this is limited to differences in
transport, as both Cowi and Artelia generally treat biomass as carbon neutral [13]. As such,
this recommendation is technically followed. The impact of this on the EF is expected to be
minimal though, due to the mentioned limitations.
Environmental and economic optimals
12
==PAGE:21==
2.2. Emission factors
Third party verification: Artelias EF’s are third party verified by ”Teknologisk Institut” in the fol­
lowing aspects: conformity between purpose, use and target audience; system boundary; rep­
resentativeness of used datasets, LCA methodical assumptions and limitations when modelling
background EF’s. It is stated that this is done as a critical LCA review [13].
Update within 5 years: As Artelia’s calculations came out in 2023 only 3 years passed between
the two reports. As such, this recommendation is followed [13].
Towards the end of Artelia’s report, a number of reflections are made. These include, but are
not limited to:
A need to calculate the difference between roof and field PV’s.
An assessment of the impact of Guarantees of Origin on the EF, as these may lead to
double counting after the sale of electricity and DH.
The assessment is based on already accepted investments and political goals. The po­
litical goals dominate the projection when looking further into the future. As such, if these
goals are not realised, the calculated EF’s will not be correct.
It is recommended that the EF’s are recalculated every 2nd year, or if major changes occur
in the energy projections.
As such, while the recommendations made by Cowi were mostly followed, there is still uncer­
tainties in the calculations that need further work. The time between each recommended report
is also significantly shorter in Artelia’s case. This could signify that changes in the projections,
and therefore future EF’s, may change more rapidly than earlier predictions.
Electricity projections
Both calculations include a major expansion in wind and solar energy. According to ”Danmarks
Energifremskrivning 2018”, used by Cowi, wind is providing 68.5% of the total electricity produc­
tion by 2040 with solar energy providing a 17.4%. The remaining production is mostly through
biomass and trash in co­generation plants. The remaining production types are negligible with
the amount produced each year shown in Figure 2.2.
According to AF22 wind and solar are also by far domineering the electricity production at the
end of its period in 2050. While the exact split is not provided Figure 2.4 shows that it is by far
most of the production, with the last minor contributions being from biomass and trash.
As such the calculations appear to agree on the main producers, although Artelia expects a
quicker and more comprehensive use of wind and solar electricity. The assumptions made for
solar electricity appear very similar with the same types of PV used, although there are minor
differences in the ratio. Artelia does not count roof based PV to avoid double counting and it is
not known whether Cowi does the same.
Regarding wind energy no major differences can be seen between the two calculations, with
the exception being that Artelia appears to have a more detailed split between ocean and land
based wind turbines. Cowi does not include a clear ratio between the two types of wind turbines
contribution.
The main driver between the differences in electricity’s EF is therefore expected to be from the
co­generation plant. This difference is of significant importance as Cowi includes emissions
from the incineration of both trash and biomass, while Artelia treats both as carbon neutral
outside of processing and transport.
The last major difference is the inclusion of import and export of electricity in Artelia’s calcula­
tions. Whether this increases or decreases their EF depends on the year as it differs how much
Environmental and economic optimals
13
==PAGE:22==
2.2. Emission factors
is imported and from where.
Due to these differences it is assumed that Artelia’s calculations result in a more realistic EF for
electricity, with the caveat being that there are many parts assumed carbon neutral. As such the
resulting EF is most likely too low each year, with the actual EF being somewhere in­between
the two calculations, leaning towards Artelia’s due to the higher detail level.
DH
The projected sources for DH vary greatly between the two projections. ”Danmarks Energifrem­
skrivning 2018” includes a major heat generation from co­generation power plants, mostly
biomass and trash, followed by boilers incinerating biomass and trash.
AF22 on the other hand projects a large scale electrification with more minor contributions from
biomass and biogenic trash. As both of these sources are treated as climate neutral, outside of
processing and transport, this contribution is most likely minor. As such the EF for DH is largely
reliant on the electricity’s EF for Artelia, which was discussed above.
These major differences in projected DH production results in the large difference in EF shown
in Figure 2.1. Due to the generally higher detail level in Artelia’s calculations it is assumed that
these are closer to the actual EF, although the significant parts of the production being treated
as climate neutral is artificially lowering the EF. As such the assumption will be kept that the
actual EF is somewhere between the two calculations. Which one is most correct will likely
depend on the degree of electrification in the DH production.
In process recalculation of EF
As of writing Rune Andersen, Assistant Professor at DTU Sustain, is recalculating the EF based
on Artelia’s assumptions. The project is not complete and it has therefore not been possible to
see the exact calculations, but he has shared the EF’s he has currently calculated. These EF’s
are as such subject to change. This is shown along with the official EF’s from Cowi and Artelia
in Figure 2.6.
Figure 2.6: EF calculated by Cowi, Artelia and Rune Andersen
These results agree with the assumption that the EF of DH is between the EF’s from Cowi and
Artelia. The recalculated EF for electricity is much closer, actually exceeding, the EF calculated
by Cowi. As these results are subject to change they will not be officially used in the thesis, but
do support the assumption that the real EF for DH is somewhere between Cowi’s and Artelia’s.

2.3. Levelised Cost of Carbon Abatement
The recalculated electricity EF being slightly outside the range is deemed of minor importance,
as all buildings simulated are heated through DH. As such the main impact of the electricity’s
EF is in the electrification of DH, which is included in the EF of DH already.
The assumption is that the actual future EF’s are somewhere between Cowi’s and Artelia’s cal­
culations. These are therefore used as an upper and lower limit with the actual results some­
where in between.
2.3
Levelised Cost of Carbon Abatement
An issue that arises when deciding between policy based decisions to limit GHGe is having to
decide between them. Friedmann et al mentions that policies that appear effective often have
little impact or are more costly than alternatives [15]. To help decision makers avoid this issue
they set forward the concept of Levelized Cost of Carbon Abatement (LCCA). The concept aims
to improve the understanding of real climate costs and benefits. To do this it includes specific
and local CO2 emissions for all estimations and applies standard financial metrics to represent
and compare costs consistently.
2.3.1
Methodology
To make use of LCCA any value must be represented as a monetary value, e.g. Euro, USD
or DKK, per unit of realized or estimated carbon abatement [15]. In a Danish context the most
common unit would therefore be:
DKK/kg CO2 −eq.
For a LCCA to be valid Friedmann states that there must be an actual reduction or removal of
GHGe. As such a replacement of a hypothetical cause of emissions would not be valid, but a
real or hypothetical replacement of an existing cause would be. There is also an economic part
of LCCA which should include the basic cost of implementation, operating cost and tear­down
cost, or the cost of implementing a new policy. Another important aspect is that the economic
perspective should be from a socioeconomic perspective. Incentives such as tax exemptions
should therefore not be included as that part will simply be paid by a different institution than
the primary purchaser [15].
If these requirements are followed a simple estimate of the LCCA (L) can be made using the
equation:
L = C/(E0 −E1)
Where C is the cost associated with the change, E0 is the GHGe in tonne from the original
configuration and E1 is the GHGe emissions in tonne of the new configuration [15].
Due to the nature of this equation Friedmann notes a few observations:
1. If E1 = E0 then the denominator is zero leading to an infinite LCCA which means the
transaction fails to achieve climate benefits.
2. If E1 < E0 there will be a fractional reduction in the cost of the transaction.
3. If E1 = 0 there is a 100% reduction in emissions, leading to the cost being divided by the
emissions and LCCA is the cost of the transaction per tonne.
4. If E1 < 0 it is added to E0 leading to a further decrease in LCCA.
5. If C < 0 money is saved in the transaction resulting in a negative L.
6. If E1 > E0 then the new approach results in more emissions than before, resulting in a
climate failure that is not suitable for LCCA.
Environmental and economic optimals
15
==PAGE:24==
2.3. Levelised Cost of Carbon Abatement
As can be seen in point 5 above it is possible for the economic cost to be negative. According
to Friedmann et al this is perfectly acceptable and could for example be caused by efficiency
improvements, switching fuel sources or replacements [15].
2.3.2
Comparison of LCCA results
The method is suited for both static and dynamic aspects of CO2 reductions, and is generally
good to calculate how much CO2 reduction you can get for your money. This is also the primary
appeal of the method. Using LCCA it is possible to make ”apples to apples” comparisons [15]
which is not possible to do using standard LCA as the functional units differ drastically. Like
most tools they do state that this is not an ”end all be all” argument, but simply useful to make
accurate comparisons between various policies [15].
Friedmann et al made some illustrative scenarios and a few other papers have also utilized
LCCA. Some of the results can be seen in Table 2.6.

caused by rounding errors, changes in the exchange rates or small variations in the assump­
tions. Furthermore, direct air capture and cement production have relatively low ambient CO2
concentrations. This is reflected in the economic costs as the commentary shows high ambient
CO2 concentration sectors to have a much lower LCCA values [16]. An example of this is ”CCS,
natural gas processing” which is much cheaper despite being essentially the same technology.

ample of this is Skribbe’s study into substituting conventional ammonia production with power­

to­ammonia technologies. The study showed a quick peak in LCCA driven by a high initial
investment cost and demand for ammonia leading to import. This high value then quickly starts
to decrease before stabilising at the lower value at around the year 2045 [17]. As such the
value is not always stable and it is always relevant to consider a broader perspective. This only
reiterates Friedmann et als statement that LCCA is not an end all argument but rather a useful
tool for decision making [15].
Regarding the costs Friedmann et al states that hard­to­abate sectors can be defined as when
more than 75% of CO2 reductions in the sector cost more than 200 USD/tonne under reason­
able assumptions. Using the exchange rate for USD shown in Table 2.6 the equivalent is 1386
DKK. This does not necessarily mean that sectors such as ”power­to­ammonia” are hard to
abate because of its high upper cost. A sector such as ”Sustainable Aviation Fuels” however,
could be considered hard­to­abate as the lower cost is still above the threshold.
2.4
Optimisation of insulation
A number of studies looking into optimal insulation thicknesses around the world have already
been made. They typically focus on limiting lifetime emissions or cost. To find potential gaps in
the literature five of these studies will be presented and looked into. They vary in geographic
location, timeline and have different reasons for the study being made, but are looking into some
kind of optimisation of insulation thickness in buildings.
2.4.1
Optimal insulation in Denmark 2005
The first relevant paper introduced is by Henrik Tommerup and Svend Svendsen. In 2005
they investigated the most cost­effective way of complying with the then new requirements for
insulation. They investigated 3 insulation thicknesses in the exterior walls, being 125, 200 and
250 mm with a Thermal Conductivity (TC) of 0.037W/mK. For the terrain deck EPS with a TC
of 0.038W/mK was used [18]. The resulting thicknesses are illustrated in Table 2.7.
Wall [mm]
Ceiling [mm]
Terrain deck [mm]
Additional cost [DKK]

80000

61000

Table 2.7: Optimal insulation thicknesses according to cost Denmark 2005 [18]
These thicknesses take details on the ventilation system into account, with some further options
for the costs mentioned. All results are, however, the minimum thicknesses to comply with the
energy requirements of the time, and as such GHGe have no influence on the decision making
in this paper. To include this other studies are needed.
2.4.2
Optimisation of thermal insulation thickness in Norway and Greenland
2023
In 2023 Jørn Emil Gaarder et al studied the optimal insulation thicknesses in the exterior wall in
several locations in both Norway and Greenland. They accounted for both the embodied and
operational emissions, and it was found that the EF of the energy was the main driver of the
optimal thickness [11]. Most of their cases, with exception of case 3, where made by modelling
1 square metre of exterior wall with both a historical context (1961­1990) and a future context
(Representative Concentration Pathway (RCP) 8.5. 2071­2100). Case 3 was a full theoretical
Environmental and economic optimals
17
==PAGE:26==
2.4. Optimisation of insulation
building modelled to test the validity of their results. The heat losses have been calculated either
based on a critical outdoor temperature or via Heating Degree Days (HDD). Wall constructions
were further made to match a typical wall in the location of the case and as such varies between
wooden framework for Case 1 and 3. and a concrete wall for Case 2 [11]. The resulting optimal
thicknesses are presented in Table 2.8.
Location

Table 2.8: Optimal insulation thicknesses from Gaarder et al [11]
As can be seen the optimal thicknesses vary greatly, even within the same country. The main
reason for the largest difference, Greenland, is the highly varied EF used. As Greenland does
not have a centralised electricity grid the available sources vary greatly from location to location.
Nuuk is both located south of the polar circle and has access to hydropower leading to a less
extreme climate and a very low EF. Aasiaat on the other hand is further north and is reliant on
fossil fuels leading to a much higher EF [11].
The differences in the Norwegian cases on the other hand are driven by the more complicated
model in Case 3, The inclusion of a ventilation system, passive heating from electrical equipment
and solar gains resulted in similar behaviour for both cases, but less pronounced in the full model
[11].
The main conclusion was that climate change will reduce the optimal insulation thickness of
buildings, with a significant influence from occupant behaviour. In cold climates the insulation
thickness depends greatly on the EF of energy, with factors above 25 −30g CO2 −eq./kWh
resulting in thicknesses above 500mm (the studies limit)[11].
Another relevant finding was that the consequences of missing this impact varied greatly across
the cases. In the Norwegian cases missing the optimum by 100mm resulted in an emission in­
crease of less than 5%. In Greenland, however, missing the optimum by 50mm resulted in a
significant increase in emissions [11].
2.4.3
The relationship between operational energy demand and embodied
energy in the Netherlands
Another study, by Anastasia Koezjakov et al in 2018, looked into the relationship between op­
erational and embodied energy in Dutch residential buildings. They mention that, due to the
high global energy demand for heating and cooling, the GHGe from the built environment have
been identified to have the highest potential in reductions. Reducing these often leads to an
increase in embodied energy use, which are more complex and time consuming to assess than
the operational energy use [19]. While this thesis does not deal directly with embodied energy
it is still considered a relevant comparison, as its relationship with operational energy is similar
to that of embodied and operational emissions.
As part of the study an assessment was made of the trade­off between operational energy use
and embodied energy use, to allow decision makers to move towards optimisation of perfor­
mance. To do this they created scenario based models for various building archetypes in the
Dutch building typology including when and how they were built. These range from buildings
Environmental and economic optimals
18
==PAGE:27==
2.4. Optimisation of insulation
typically built in 1965­1974, buildings built according to the Dutch 2015 regulations and their
nearly­Zero Energy Building standard. All simulations run up to 2050 and only takes heating
into account, as cooling is hardly necessary in the Netherlands at present, and there are no
insights as to when it will make up a relevant amount of operational energy use [19].
The study found that the operational energy is much higher than the embodied energy, with this
being much more clear for older buildings which use less insulation. For more modern buildings,
and especially the nearly­Zero Energy Buildings, the embodied energy is higher resulting in a
much lower operational energy use. In total the study found that while the embodied energy is
higher, the total energy use had a reduction of 63­66% when compared to standard dwellings.
The exact reduction depends on the building typology, but the general conclusion is that the
embodied energy will become much more important.
2.4.4
Energy demand for heating and cooling in residential housing, Finland
Kirsti Jylhä et al made a paper in 2015 on the operational energy demand in a Finnish context.
Focus was on detached houses with hourly weather data for 2030, 2050 and 2100 and found
that the demand for heating decreased by 20­40% while cooling demand increased by 40­80%
[20].
More than 80% of the total energy consumption in Finland is spent on heating, with common
methods being DH, wood and electricity. To investigate this consumption in the future they
used a dynamic building simulation program, IDA ICE, and climate change projections. Fu­
ture revisions to building regulations or improvements in energy performance were not taken
into account. To account for the changing climate from the projections they modified existing
weather files with expected monthly changes in temperature and the standard deviation of the
temperature. As such the changes were not uniformly distributed but rather most significant
during the peak of the heating season [20].
Besides the energy consumption they also looked at the Net Present Value (NPV) using aver­
age actual prices in 2011­2012 for electricity and DH. A yearly increase in these prices where
included as a 3% increase for DH and a 2% increase for electricity. This resulted in the cost for
DH decreasing by 11­16% by 2100. from an expected NPV of 1300euro/m2 when not including
climate change. The cost of electricity however, will increase, although not by as much [20].
In conclusion the study found that the annual heating demand will decrease at a rate of 2­4% per
decade, while the annual heating demand will increase at a rate of 4­8% per decade. This does
result in savings especially if heat is provided via DH and cooling via electricity. Using dynamic
building simulations over Cooling Degree Days (CDD) gives further value as it provides more
detailed results.
2.4.5
Optimal external wall insulation thicknesses in Turkey 2020
Until now the papers discussed generally were in either temperate or colder climates. Insulation
optimisation is however also relevant in warmer climates. An example of one such paper is by Ö.
Altan Dombayci et al. In 2020 they made a paper determining the optimum insulation thickness
for external walls in Turkey [21].
Turkey is very dependant on imported energy and reducing energy usage is therefore important
to decrease this dependency in the energy sector. Insulating to decrease energy consumption is
one of the most effective solutions, while also reducing temperature fluctuations and increasing
indoor comfort which is also important [21].
To achieve this a degree­day method to measure the annual consumption of natural gas, the
most common heating method in Turkey, was used. They then used a sandwich wall consisting
Environmental and economic optimals
19
==PAGE:28==
2.4. Optimisation of insulation
of plaster, bricks and insulation material. To find the optimal thickness they varied its thickness
and measured the total environmental impact. By equation the derivative of that to zero the
study found the optimal thickness to be 400mm for glass wool and 180mm for stone wool [21].
2.4.6
Comparison
The various papers show a variety of reasons for their studies, such as reducing dependency of
imports, economic costs and minimising GHGe. This can also be seen in the units used, mean­
ing that very few of the studies can be compared directly. This highlights the point mentioned
in Section 2.3 of providing an apples to apples comparison. This would of course require addi­
tional work for the majority of the papers, as most of the studies only deal with either economic
costs or environmental perspectives.
Two of the studies can be directly compared though. Both Gaarder and Dombayci provide direct
optimal thicknesses in their results. These both agree with the trend Gaarder mentioned, being
that thicknesses are highly driven by the EF of the energy source. More specifically, the differ­
ences in thickness between Nuuk, Greenland and Turkey are minimal with 250mm and 180mm
respectively [11, 21]. This is despite stark differences in climates, but the higher emission factor
of natural gas over hydropower offsets the difference. Gaarder’s model of Aasiaat supports this,
as it needs requires a higher insulation thickness than is realistic to reach an optimum, despite
being a similar climate as Nuuk.
While Tommerups paper does conclude on optimal thicknesses it cannot be directly compared
to the prior two papers as the goals are not comparable. While Gaarder and Dombayci found
the optimal thicknesses for the exterior walls of the building taking the environmental impact
into consideration [11, 21] Tommerup focused on the economic side only [18]. He further inves­
tigated both the roof, terrain deck and exterior walls to find the economic optimum for all parts
[18]. This study focused only on new buildings following the energy regulations of the time and
as such does not incorporate the temporal scale. This is included in Jylhäs study which found
that climate change resulted in a lower heating demand [20]. While these results are from a
Finnish context it can still reasonably be assumed that the Danish demand for heating will also
decrease due to climate change. Therefore, including the temporal factor will be very relevant.
These expected changes to the operational energy, and therefore emissions, lead into the re­
lationship between operational and embodied emissions. While it, as mentioned does not deal
directly with emissions Koezjakov’s study found that the operational energy is usually much
higher than the embodied energy [19]. Multiple of the studies [11, 19, 18] deal with the trade­
off between operational and embodied emissions to some extent. And according to Koezjakov
knowledge about this trade­off will allow decision makers to take steps towards optimising the
performance of a building over its lifetime. This is especially important in colder climates due to
the diminishing effect of higher insulation thicknesses [11] that is showcased in Figure 2.7.
Environmental and economic optimals
20
==PAGE:29==
2.4. Optimisation of insulation
Figure 2.7: U­values resulting from insulation material thickness
As can be seen the highest impact on the thermal resistance comes at lower thicknesses leading
to diminishing effects at higher thicknesses. While not directly mentioned it is very likely that it
is this effect that leads to Aasiaat’s results being OoB [11]. This is also true from an economic
perspective where the cost of the first 100mm of insulation is the same as the last 100mm.
The heat loss prevented by the initial insulation, however, is much higher than the last. It is
essentially this relationship Tommerup investigated when doing their analysis on the economic
optimum [18].
Environmental and economic optimals
21
==PAGE:30==
3
Research questions
3.1
Research gaps
Through the literature study in Section 2 a number of research gaps have been found. These
gaps include:
1. The projected EF’s for Denmark vary significantly. As the operational emissions depend
heavily on these, it could lead to wrong results if an incorrect EF is used.
2. The current optimal insulation thickness in Denmark is not known in either an economic
nor environmental capacity, as the only paper found is from an outdated version of the
building regulations.
3. The effect of expected climate changes is often neglected, despite papers frequently ac­
knowledging its potential impact on the results.
While these gaps cannot all be addressed through a single thesis, they are still important to
take into account. Another important point to take into consideration is the fact that direct com­
parisons were rarely possible. While this is not a research gap it was a general issue that was
encountered during the literature study, caused by the various units and goals of the papers.
Utilising a method that allows easier comparisons would enable decision makers to make more
effective choices in the future.
3.2
Hypothesis
With the goal of finding the optimal insulation thicknesses in a Danish context, and based on
the found research gaps, 5 hypotheses were made. These hypotheses were made to widely
cover the found research gaps, and their potential impacts on the results. As such each should
impact the optimal insulation thickness for at least one part of the building envelope for Danish
houses.
1. There is an optimal insulation thickness in the current climate of Denmark.
Currently an optimal insulation thickness for Denmark is not known. Despite that requirements
for LCA for all building projects has recently been included in BR18, along with the existing
energy requirements. These requirements provide minimum requirements for heat loss through
each part of the building envelope, for both new and renovated buildings. As such knowing the
optimum would aid in deciding what these minimum requirements should be in the future.
2. Projected changes to the climate and energy EF will reduce the optimal thickness.
The projected changes in the climate will likely result in a lowered need for heating in Denmark.
Meanwhile the projected EF of energy will make it cheaper, at least environmentally, to heat
resulting in an expected decrease in the optimal insulation thickness for Denmark.
3. The economic and environmental optimal insulation thickness differs significantly.
The economic optimal insulation thickness depends on the cost of insulation and DH rather than
the GHGe. As such the economic and environmental optimums may differ significantly. This
difference will have a high impact on the economic cost or gain associated with limiting GHGe
from Danish buildings, and is as such highly significant when deciding between policies.
4. The impact of missing the optimal thickness lead to minor changes in GHGe.
The impact of missing the optimal thickness by 100mm varies from case to case. With lower
EF’s and Denmark’s temperate climate it is expected that missing this optimum by 100mm in
 Environmental and economic optimals
22
==PAGE:31==
3.2. Hypothesis
either direction will not result in significantly increased emissions. If this is the case there may
be a relevant middle ground between an economic and environmental optimum.
5. Using 3 pane windows is environmentally better than 2 pane windows.
With the current requirements for windows, 3 pane windows are indirectly required in Danish
buildings. While they do typically limit the operational emissions more than a 2 pane window,
they will also result in more embodied emissions. It is expected that the value of the avoided
operational emissions exceed the added embodied emissions regardless of the window area in
the building.
Environmental and economic optimals
23
==PAGE:32==
4
Method
The study is built upon five pillars being the case buildings, the weather, emission factors for
electricity and DH, emissions from mineral wool, and the price of electricity and heating in the
future. These five pillars along with the main variables within each are shown in Figure 4.1
followed by a short description of each pillar. Four of the pillars are further split into three time
periods, being a contemporary period set in 2025, a medium­term future set in 2050 and a long
term future set in 2075.
Figure 4.1: Main variables for for the optimisation
Case buildings: This pillar contains the data for the buildings investigated. Most of this will be
kept constant for each year, e.g. the ventilation system, interior walls and internal gains. The
insulation thickness for each component of the building envelope, exterior walls, roof and terrain
deck, will be varied between 25 −500mm while the other components are kept at a constant
thickness. Lastly the window to floor ratio will be modified by simulating three different ratios
keeping the insulation thickness of all components constant.
Weather files: Future weather files are used in the simulation for a medium­term and long­term
future. For the current optimal a recent historical weather file is used. To ensure consistency
all weather files used are from the same source.
 Environmental and economic optimals
24
==PAGE:33==
4.1. Case Buildings
Embodied emissions: The embodied emissions are limited to the insulation used in the compo­
nent that is investigated. As such only the volume of variable insulation is included. For future
embodied emissions the generic data is modified using predicted changes in GHGe for Danish
materials. Otherwise the same method is used.
Operational emissions: For the exterior walls, roof and terrain deck the operational emissions
cover the heat flux during heating for the building part. This heat flux is assumed to be constant
for the full lifetime.
For the windows the heat flux during heating for the wall, windows and line losses around win­
dows and doors are used. This ensures that larger window areas include a corresponding
reduction in the area of the exterior wall.
Life Cycle Cost (LCC): The LCC is calculated including the cost of insulation, the cost of heating
based on the heat flux during heating, and the End Of Life (EOL) cost for the insulation. It is
calculated using a calculation period covering the full lifetime, 50 years, and a shorter calculation
period of 30 years.
4.1
Case Buildings
The first major decision was determining which building typologies are most relevant. According
to Tozan [6], the highest shares for both RC and GHGe in the future are from MF houses and SF
houses. The study maps the expected construction between 2024 and 2050, and some of their
results can be seen in Table 4.1. Here the expected new construction area for each building
typology is shown along with the average GHGe and RC. Lastly the percentage share for both
RC and GHGe is shown.

SF and MF houses together are responsible for almost 50% of all GHGe and RC in this time
frame. As such, two case buildings will be set up. One for SF houses and one for MF houses.
BR18 provides examples of new construction for both SF [22] and MF [23] houses which are
both directly used as the case buildings for the three time periods. These buildings are made
as realistic as possible and simulated in the dynamic building simulation program IDA ICE.
Environmental and economic optimals
25
==PAGE:34==
4.1. Case Buildings
As cooling is currently not widely utilised in Denmark, and no data exist on the influence and
use in the future, it is not included in this thesis. As such, ideal coolers are used to limit potential
over­temperatures, and the energy used for it is ignored. This also applies to solar heat gains,
which are also ignored, as are potential mitigation methods such as exterior window shading.
4.1.1
Single Family house
The layout used for the SF house is provided by BR18 as ”BR18 basis house” [22]. The lay­
out is provided by the Danish Transport, Construction and Housing Authority, and is stated
to follow the given laws in BR18. Despite this the total energy requirements, assuming that
DH is used for heating, is 51.1kWh/m2/year [22]. This exceeds the limit from BR18 §259 by
14.4kWh/m2/year. Improvements are therefore needed to comply with this requirement. As
the thesis includes simulating variable insulation thicknesses this is not treated as an issue, and
the provided baseline is used without modification.
Layout
The SF house is a one storey construction with a heated floor area of 149.6m2. It has a living
room, a dining area, 4 bedrooms, a kitchen, a utility room and 2 bathrooms. The floor plan is
shown on Figure 4.2 and the facades are shown on Figure 4.3.
Figure 4.2: SF house floor plan

4.1. Case Buildings
Figure 4.3: SF house facade plan
Building envelope
A main driver of heat loss is the building envelope. The ”BR18 basis house” does provide a
short description of the constructions of the roof, exterior walls, terrain deck, interior walls and
line losses for the building envelope. Only the main components are included in the construction
for the sake of simplicity, as no interior surfaces are mentioned.
Roof: The house has an insulated roof with a U­value of 0.10W/m2K [22]. The construction
used to reach this U­value is shown in Table 4.2.

According to BR18 the maximum U­value of the roof/roof construction is 0.20W/m2K [9]. This
is reached at about 175mm insulation in the construction, and as such this is used as the mini­
mum thickness. The maximum thickness is set to 500mm. This value is arbitrary but chosen as
thicknesses above this are deemed unrealistic and as shown in Figure 2.7 insulation thickness
for all types have reached highly diminished returns.

According to BR18 the maximum U­value allowed for the terrain deck is 0.20W/m2K [9]. With
the same layer of expanded clay aggregate 80mm of insulation is needed to reach this value. To
comply with the increments used in this thesis a thickness of 75mm will be used as the minimum
value, despite resulting in a U­value of 0.21W/m2K. For consistency the maximum thickness
for the terrain deck insulation is also kept at 500mm.
Interior walls: The example house states that interior walls are made using lightweight con­
crete. A thickness is never stated but is kept at 100mm for all interior walls.
Line losses: The example house does contain a little bit of information on line losses. It is stated
that the exterior wall foundation is made using 2 expanded clay blocks with 100mm insulated
cores resulting in a line loss of 0.13W/mK [22]. This is used directly in the IDA ICE simulation.
It is further stated that the exterior wall includes a 50mm thermal bridge interruption for all doors
and windows. As there is no exact description of the interruption it is assumed that the framing
has an overlap of at least 20mm. As such the line losses for doors and windows are assumed
to be 0.01W/mK in accordance to DS418 [24].

According to the baseline house it is equipped with energy class B windows. This is represented
by a 2 pane window shown in Table 4.5 along with a 3 pane energy class A window. More details
for each window can be seen in Appendix A.1.

Equipment gains
The internal gains from equipment are based on the electricity usage in the building. This is
not evenly spread around the building as rooms do not all serve the same function. To give a
realistic equipment usage each room will be assigned a share of the total electricity based on
the room’s function. This split is based on the average distribution for a 4 person home [25].
The total electricity usage for new SF buildings can be approximated based on the formula:
1780kWh + A ∗17kWh [25]
Where A is the floor area. In this case that is 149.6m2 leading to a total electricity usage of
4323.2kWh resulting in a total average equipment heat gain of 493.5W. This is distributed
between 12 categories shown in Figure 4.4
Figure 4.4: Percentage distribution of electricity consumption end use in households of 4 people
from SBi 2005:12 [25]
The total electricity usage is distributed according to these categories in either area based
shares or as individual shares. Area bases shares rely on the floor area of each room and
includes: Lights and Standby. The area share is calculated according to the formula:
Area share = (Lights + Standby) ∗Aroom/Atotal
The individual shares are typically specific pieces of equipment and can as such be placed in the
typical room it is found in. It is assumed that fridges and freezers of all kind, and the dishwasher,
is placed in the kitchen. The washing machine and dryer is placed in the utility room. Lastly
Environmental and economic optimals
30
==PAGE:39==
4.1. Case Buildings
the TV+Video+HiFi is spread evenly across the 4 bedrooms and the living room. In addition it
is assumed that EDB equipment is in the living room.
The category ”Remainder” is a bit more unique as it is split between being an area based share
and an individual share. It is assumed that 20% is the stove/oven, which is placed as an in­
dividual share in the kitchen. The remaining 11% is calculated as an area based share. The
resulting room gains for the SF house can be seen in Table 4.9.

The last thing included for realistic gains is temporal variance which will be included by using
a duck profile schedule. This schedule will on average have 100% scaling to ensure the full
equipment gain is used. During the night less electricity will be used as the occupants are
assumed to be sleeping, as such the scaling is set to 70% from midnight to 5 o’clock. To
compensate scaling is set to 130% from 16­21 o’clock, resulting in an average scaling of 100%.
As such the total equipment gains during each day are kept at the calculated values.
Ventilation
The SF basic house specifies a ventilation system with a total supply of 45L/s and an exhaust
volume of 50L/s from the kitchen and bathrooms. The ventilation system has a heat recovery
system with an efficiency of 85%, a SEL of 800J/m3, an electrical heating plate and a supply
temperature of 18 degrees [22]. This barely complies with BR18s minimum requirements for
ventilation which in §443 are specified to be 0.30l/s/m2 [9]. It is, however, not specified how or
where the supply ducts are located and therefore a number of assumptions are made.
Exhaust ducts are placed in the kitchen, bathrooms and the utility room. While the utility room is
not mentioned in the documentation of the basic SF house [22] it is assumed reasonable as the
washer/dryer was placed there in Section 4.1.1. It is further assumed that the supply ducts are
not evenly spaced per square metre, as it is a quite open layout and some rooms have a higher
need. As such focus is on the bedrooms, the dining, and the living room, as these are where the
occupants will spend most of their time. For the bedrooms a ventilation rate of 5L/(s ∗person)
is used. The final supply and exhaust rates for each room can be seen in Table 4.10.

In addition to these rates, a kitchen hood is also placed in the kitchen with a ventilation rate
of 60L/s. This system has a SEL value of 1000, does not include heat recovery and is active
every day from 18:00­19:12. Due to technical limitations in IDA ICE only allowing one ventilation
system per room the normal exhaust from the kitchen is instead simulated in the dining room.
The influence of this is expected to be minimal as the two rooms are directly connected with a
large opening as shown in Figure 4.2.
The last thing taken into account related to ventilation is the infiltration. According to the docu­
mentation for the house infiltration is set to 0.5l/s/m2 at 50 Pa [22]. This is included directly in
the simulations for all scenarios.
Occupants and schedules:
Schedules are used to represent the usage of the house. Three schedules are used for the
various doors in the building and four are used for the various zones. The door schedules are
presented first followed by the occupant schedules. All schedules are based on the example
shown in DS/EN 15665:2009 Annex B [26].
Closed between 23­07: This schedule is used for the 4 bedroom doors leading to either the
hallway or the living room. These doors are set to only be closed when the occupants are
expected to be sleeping from 23 in the evening to 07 in the morning.
Never open: The exterior doors are set to never be open to ensure an enclosed envelope.
Always open: The remaining doors, being the ones between the entryway, hallway, and the
one between the living and dining room, are set to always be open.
Sleeping: The 4 bedrooms are expected to be occupied during the night from 23­06 and oth­
erwise empty [26]. The 3 bedrooms have 1 occupant and the master bedroom has 2.
Living room: The example from DS/EN 15665:2009 does not contain a dining room. As such
it is assumed that the living room is used for dining in the example and that this schedule is
therefore used for both the living and dining room. This scenario is slightly more complicated in
its timings and is as such shown in Figure 4.5.
Environmental and economic optimals
32
==PAGE:41==
4.1. Case Buildings
Figure 4.5: Living room schedule [26]
As can be seen the time where the occupation is at 100% does fit with what is considered lunch

although exact times are not followed [26].

4.1.2
Multi family home
For the MF house the ”BR18 basis apartment” is used [23]. The layout is provided by the
Danish Transport, Construction and Housing Authority. According to the provided data it has an
energy demand of 56.9kWh/m2year. Similar to the SF house, this is above the allowed energy
demand according to BR18. As modifications to the building envelope are made, this is treated
as acceptable.
Layout
The MF house is a 3 storey building with 6 small apartments of 66m2 and 6 large apartments at
91m2 [23]. It is not mentioned which rooms are in the apartments but it is assumed that the small
apartments have an entryway, a bedroom, a bathroom and a combined kitchen dining room. The
larger apartments are assumed to have an additional bedroom, for a total of 2 bedrooms.

Equipment gains
The total electricity usage for new apartment buildings can be approximated based on the for­
mula [25]:
560kWh + A ∗16kWh
Where A is the floor area. For the MF house this results in 1616kWh for the small apartments
and 2016kWh for the larger.
For the distribution of internal gains in the apartments a similar method as the SF house is
used. The only difference included is that the share for washer/dryer is not included, with the
remaining shares compensating. The resulting distribution can be seen in Figure 4.9. The
reason for this modification is the apartment layout shown in 4.7 in which there is not enough
space for a washer/dryer in the bathrooms. As such it is assumed that the washing of clothes
is not done in the apartment, but rather at a nearby laundromat. This does not result in lower
internal gains and only modifies the distribution in the apartments slightly.
Figure 4.9: Modified percentage distribution of electricity end use in households of 4 people
from SBi 2005:12 [25]

that the difference in the distribution of the equipment gains is similar. The resulting room gains
due to equipment has been calculated in the same way as the SF house and can be seen in

Ventilation
The MF house is supplied with mechanical ventilation with exhaust ducts in the bedrooms and

Occupants and schedules
It is assumed that the small apartment has 2 occupants and the large one has 3. Similar sched­
ules to the SF house are used, with the same 3 schedules for the doors and similar schedules
for the occupants.
Closed between 23­07: This schedule is used for the bedroom doors leading to the hallway.
These doors are set to only be closed when the occupants are expected to be sleeping from 23
in the evening to 07 in the morning.
Never open: The exterior doors are set to never be open to ensure an enclosed envelope.
Similarly the bathroom doors are also always kept closed.
Always open: The remaining doors, being between the entryway and the Kitchen/Dining room
for both sizes of apartment are always kept open.
Sleeping: The bedrooms are expected to be occupied during the night from 23­06 and other­
wise empty. The main bedroom in each apartment has 2 occupants with the second bedroom
in the large apartment housing an additional one occupant.
Kitchen/Living room: For the combined Kitchen/Living room the schedules for the Kitchen and
Living rooms shown in the SF house have been combined. As such when someone is present
in at least one of the schedules they will be treated as present in the combined schedule.
Figure 4.10: Kitchen/Living room schedule
Bathroom: The bathrooms in the MF home utilise the same schedule as the SF home, as
shown in Section 4.1.1.
4.2
Weather files
To simulate the impact of climate change on future energy demand weather data is required.
Building simulation programs, such as IDA ICE, typically use EPW files that provide hour by
hour data for a range of variables such as dry bulb temperature, humidity, solar radiation etc.
Typically historical files are used, based on real measured data at an hourly temporal resolution.
Files such as these are typically made by an expert at a meteorological institute or at a dedicated
research centre. In Denmark the most typically used files are either Design Reference Year
(DRY) or Typical Reference Year (TRY) files, based on measurements between 1961­1990 [27].
To properly incorporate the most average year of a time period a full year of measurements are
typically not used. Rather each year in a time period is split into intervals, e.g. a month, and the
mean for each interval is then compared to the other years. The interval with the closest match
is then chosen and inserted into the final file. As such the file consists of average intervals to
ensure short heatwaves or especially cold weeks are not treated as normal [27].
While weather files based on more recent data, such as the commonly used DRY 2001 ­ 2010
dataset, does exist they do not account for future climate change. Creating future weather files
is arguably harder than historic ones. They still need to contain the same hourly data for as
many categories as the historic files, despite having no measurements as a base. The DRY
Environmental and economic optimals
39
==PAGE:48==
4.2. Weather files
2001 ­ 2010 dataset contains data on temperature, relative humidity, wind speed, wind direc­
tion, atmospheric pressure, global radiation, cloud cover, sea temperature, diffuse irradiance,
illuminance, and soil temperature for Denmark [28]. It should be noted that both the temporal
and geographical scope is very relevant as the climate naturally varies around the globe. When
including expected climate change this is further impacted as various climate zones are not
expected to be impacted by climate change in the same ways [1].
This is to say that, despite the very high interest in predicting the effects of future climate change,
future weather files are not readily available in many regions of the world [27]. This includes
Denmark where, despite extended attempts, only one set of future weather files were found.
4.2.1
Annex 80
Annex 80 is an international project on resilient cooling in the future. In their own words ”The
objective of Annex 80 is to develop, assess and communicate solutions for resilient cooling.[29]”
For this thesis the relevant part of the project is their use of dynamic building simulation in their
work. For that they have produced future weather files for 15 different cities around the world.
One of these cities is Copenhagen, which is an example of what they call a cold humid climate
zone. For each of the cities two types of weather files were made, being Typical Meteorological
Year (TMY) and Years containing heatwaves (HWY) [29]. The relevant weather files are in this
case the TMY.
For each city 3 TMY weather files were made, one for the contemporary period (2001­2020),
one for the medium­term future (2041­2060) and one for the long term future (2081­2100). Each
file is made using 12 representative months chosen from the 20 year periods [30]. The future
climate is made based on a regional climate model (CORDEX) and hourly observations of his­
torical weather for bias correction. The climate models are based on the worst case climate
scenario RCP8.5 from the Fifth Assessment Report from The Intergovernmental Panel on Cli­
mate Change (IPCC) [29].
Looking at the EPW file in IDA ICE it can be seen that the weather files contain data for Dry­
bulb­temperature, relative air humidity, direct normal radiance, diffuse radiance, wind speed,
cloudiness, precipitation and albedo for the three futures. As the main driving factor to heat loss
is the outside temperature the Dry­bulb­temperature for the futures are shown in Figure 4.11 ,
4.12 and 4.13.
20 year mean temperature for Copenhagen for the periods 2010s=8.8, 2050 is 9.7 and 2090 is
11.3. [30] Solar irradiance is falling. Stable humidity and wind speed.
Environmental and economic optimals

==PAGE:49==
4.2. Weather files
Figure 4.11: Temperature file for 2001­2020 from Annex 80 as shown in IDA ICE
The temperature for the contemporary period rarely exceeds 25oC, with negative temperatures
commonly seen from late December to mid March. The 20 year mean temperature is 8.8oC
[30].
Figure 4.12: Temperature file for 2041­2060 from Annex 80 as shown in IDA ICE
Going to the medium­term future it can be seen that the temperatures vary more in each month.
During the winter the temperature is more often above freezing than before, and spikes reaching
30oC can be seen during the summer. The 20 year mean temperature is 9.7oC, an increase of
0.9oC from before [30].
Environmental and economic optimals

==PAGE:50==
4.3. Embodied emissions
Figure 4.13: Temperature file for 2081­2100 from Annex 80 as shown in IDA ICE
In the long­term future weather file the large spikes in the summer are no longer visible. In­
stead there is a consistently higher temperature which is especially visible during the winter
as it no longer have longer periods below freezing, which is now only seen through spikes in
the temperature. The 20 year mean temperature is 11.3oC, an increase of 2.5oC from the con­
temporary file [30]. With the consistently higher temperature it is expected that the difference
in total energy requirements for the building will decrease more significantly here than for the
medium­term weather file.
4.3
Embodied emissions
For this study the preferred source of data is generic data from a Danish context. In 2023 Jesper
Kragh and Harpa Birgisdottir produced generic LCA data for Denmark at Aalborg university [31].
This data includes a large assortment of mineral wool with various thermal transmittances and
uses. The data does have a few limitations however. First and foremost it is not stated which
EPD’s the various material categories are based on. This leads to two issues, being that it is not
known whether it is glass or stone wool insulation, and that it is not known which EPD expires
first. This second part is the biggest issue as a large number of the relevant EPDs expired
in December 2024 [31] and as such are not valid at the point of writing. They do state that
this was known when the data was collected but decided to be kept unless they presented a
significant outlier. For this thesis, however, it was decided to reinvestigate the relevant EPD’s
on the market.
Relevant EPD databases were investigated for relevant EPDs.
First the Danish database
EPDdanmark was used. At the time of writing it contains 50 EPDs within the category ”In­
sulation”. After this the larger Norwegian database epd­norway was used, which at the time
of writing contains 245 EPDs in the category insulation. A majority of these are not relevant
to Denmark, but a good number is specifically within a Danish context or contain conversion
factors for a Danish context. The relevant data from these sites was compiled into the same
categories as was used in the generic LCA data for Denmark [31]. At this point the number
of found EPDs did not match the number found by Kragh. For some categories some were
missing and for some more were found. As such a mail was sent to Jesper Kragh who provided
the data for ”murbatts” (Insulation meant for brick exterior walls), flexible insulation and EPS
insulation used in the study. Comparing this to the found EPDs showed that generally the same
EPDs were found with the main difference being EPDs produced after the study and some new
Environmental and economic optimals
42
==PAGE:51==
4.3. Embodied emissions
ones produced to replace the ones expiring.
To produce the LCA emissions the same method as Kragh [31] will be used. The method is as
follows for each phase:
Phase A1­A3:
1. Emissions based on 5 or less variants will use the maximum value multiplied with 1.1 as
a safety factor.
2. Emissions based on more than 5 variants will use the 75%­fractile multiplied with 1.1 as
a safety factor.
3. Negative emissions stemming from a large fraction of biogenic materials will instead use
the maximum value multiplied by 0.9 as a safety factor.
Phase C3 and C4:
1. Emissions will always be based on the maximum value multiplied by a safety factor of 1.1
no matter the number of EPDs.
Phase D
1. Emissions will be based on the maximum value multiplied by a safety factor of 0.9,
The resulting emissions for each type of insulation can be seen in the following sections. All
used EPD’s can be seen in Appendix A.2 along with the GHGe for each category.
Mineral wool for brick walls
The first relevant category used in the study was ”murbatts”. This insulation is used in brick
walls and is separated based on its TC. As there is no more than 5 EPDs in each category the
maximum value with a safety factor will be used for all phases. This results in the following
emissions shown in Table 4.19:
