4.5. Life Cycle Cost
4.5
Life Cycle Cost
To calculate the LCC of the various buildings Net Present Cost (NPC) is used. NPC is used to
represent the NPV of relevant cost categories over the buildings lifetime. The equation used for
this is as follows:
NPC(τ) = Ci +
τ
∑
i=1
Cf,i
(1 + r)i +
∑
j
[
CEOL,τ −Vf,τ(j)
(1 + r)τ
]
Where NPC(τ) is the Net Present Cost [DKK] linked to the duration period τ [years]. Ci is the
initial investment [DKK], Cf,i is the future cost at year i [DKK], r is the real discount rate [%],
CEOL,τ is the waste handling cost of the component at the end of the calculation period [DKK],
and Vf,τ(j) is the final value of the component at the end of the calculation period [32]. Accord­
ing to Moschetti the equation is based off of ISO 15686­5:2008. As of 2017 this standard was
replaced with ISO 15686­5:2017. A final value of components is never mentioned in this stan­
dard and is therefore not included in the final calculation. The remaining parts of the equation
still fit within the standard and is therefore chosen to be included [33]. The final equation used
is as such:
NPC(τ) = Ci +
τ
∑
i=1
Cf,i
(1 + r)i +
∑
j
[
CEOL,τ
(1 + r)τ
]
For this thesis the cost will focus solely on the heating and insulation materials used in a building
with an average expected lifetime. As such the values used in the equation will take the following
into account:
τ For LCC a common calculation time is 50 years. This fits with the expected lifetime of the
building [34]. In addition an analysis using 30 years will be made to see if the results are
significantly different when looking from a more realistic time frame of ownership.
Ci The initial investment will only include the cost of insulation. It is assumed that the cost of
concrete, bricks, installation and similar will remain unchanged regardless of the insulation
thickness. As such it is not included for simplification.
Cf,i The future cost will only include the cost of heating the building. There is no expected
replacements or repairs included as the lifetime of the relevant materials is expected to
be at least 50 years.
r According to DS/ISO 15686­5:2017 discount rates between 0­4% are typically used [33].
As such a discount rate of 3% is chosen as this is recommended for a flat interest rate. It
is also used for DGNB in Denmark and as such considered realistic [34].
CEOL,τ The EOL price is set at 25% of the procurement cost. This is the standard used in LCCbyg
when no replacement of materials are occurring [35].
Material cost
As mentioned the only material included in the LCC is insulation. The current price of insulation
is used, with prices taken from Stark.dk for the relevant types if mineral wool. The calculation
of the prices can be seen in Appendix A.3. The resulting price for one cubic meter of flexible
insulation is:
λ37: 764 DKK/m3
λ34: 1252 DKK/m3
Environmental and economic optimals
46
==PAGE:55==
4.5. Life Cycle Cost
λ32: 1776 DKK/m3
As the mineral wool used differs for the wall constructions a different price is also used. Wall
insulation:
λ37: 1142 DKK/m3
λ34: 1570 DKK/m3
λ32: 1596 DKK/m3
The last insulation material used is EPS. The current price for the relevant types of EPS are:
λ38, 60kN: 458 DKK/m3
λ31, 60kN: 572 DKK/m3
λ38, 80kN: 520 DKK/m3
λ31, 80kN: 638 DKK/m3
These prices are assumed to be stable and will as such also be used for future scenarios.
As the cost of windows depend on the size a flat price cannot be used. Furthermore the price of
windows vary further depending on the type, design, functions, and is not typically readily avail­
able online. As such it was decided to use a simple top guided window. The price depending
on the window area can be seen in Figure 4.15.
Figure 4.15: Cost of top guided window based on area
While this price is very uncertain it has not been possible to obtain more suitable data. As such
it will be used as is, but economic results related to the windows should be considered critically.
Heating cost
As calculations are made using a flat interest rate the prices for heating are also kept stable [34].
As such the price of DH used will be the current data. According to key numbers from LCCbyg,
a LCC calculation tool used in the Danish construction industry, the average price of DH for a
private costumer in the period 2018­2021 was 0.53DKK/kWh [35]. This value is used for the
base cost of heating for all results.
Environmental and economic optimals
47
==PAGE:56==
5
Results and analysis
In this section the resulting optimal thicknesses for all variables will be shown. The result will
be presented split into each building part, and then further into an environmental and economic
analysis, following the structure showcased here:
• Building part
­ Environmental results SF
­ Environmental results MF
­ Comparison of environmental results
­ Economic results SF
­ Economic results MF
­ Comparison of economic results
­ LCCA results SF
­ LCCA results MF
­ Comparison of LCCA results
Each result section shown, e.g. Environmental results SF, contains three types of results.
GHGe resulting from a U­value: These figures show the total emissions from the building
component over the expected lifetime of the building based on the resulting U­value from
the insulation thickness in the building component. This is done to compare whether the
optimum varied between insulation types and time periods. A variation is made based on
the economic cost over the lifetime as well.
Table of optimal thicknesses: The optimal thickness resulting from each combination of
year and insulation type is presented for easy comparison. This is done for both the
environmental and economic optimum.
Impact from missing the optimum: These figures show how much higher the impact is
when missing the optimum. As existing literature has included this, the same thicknesses
will be used at both 50 and 100mm. Due to the method of calculating emissions from other
types of insulation these thicknesses do not work, and as such increases in emissions will
be based on 2 and 4 steps respectively.
If the resulting optimum is either the highest or lowest thickness simulated it will be considered
OoB. This is done as it is not clear whether this is the actual optimum or if it is not reached
yet. For analysis of missing the optimum the resulting NPV and GHGe from 25 and 500mm,
TC 0.037 insulation or equivalent, will be included. Values outside this range have not been
simulated and as such will naturally not be included.
The results from the variable window to floor ratio simulation follow the same method, with the
sole difference being that the shown results are dependent on the window to floor ratio rather
than the U­value.

Environmental and economic optimals
51
==PAGE:60==
5.1. Exterior wall construction
Figure 5.4: Increase in GHGe from missing the optimum insulation thickness for MF exterior
wall
The impact of missing the found optimum for the MF Exterior walls is remarkably close to Fig­
ure 5.2 which shows the same results for the SF house. A minor difference is shown for TC
0.032 2025, which aligns more with the other time periods in the MF than the SF.
Comparison
Overall optimal: The insulation type and thickness resulting in the lowest total GHGe, when
using the EF from Artelia, is 300−325mm of TC 0.037 insulation for both the SF and MF house.
Using the EF from Cowi results in results above the set limit, with all but one result being OoB.
The actual optimal thickness is therefore somewhere above 300mm for the exterior wall, with
similar results for both buildings. This is equivalent to a U­value for the exterior wall construction
of 0.118. This value is significantly lower than the minimum requirements from BR18 for both
new and renovated construction.
Variable insulation type: Comparing the different insulation types shows that a lower TC results
in a higher optimal U­value. As shown in the Figures it also results in a higher GHGe for all time
periods, with the difference being driven by the embodied emissions. As such, with the current
production methods it is not worth using insulation with a lower TC than 0.037.
Variable time periods: When comparing the different time periods a variation in optimal insu­
lation thickness is seen for both buildings. For the SF house this is an increase from 300 to
325mm in 2050, before returning to 300mmm in 2075. This is likely caused by the embodied
emissions being reduced further than the operational. For the MF house the only difference be­
tween the time periods is a reduction from 325 to 300mm in 2075, when the mean temperature
sees the most significant increase. As such this is likely caused by the decrease in operational
emissions rather than the embodied.
Impact of missing the optimum: The increase in GHGe caused by using 100mm insulation
more or less than the optimal is very similar for both buildings. Using insulation with a higher
TC generally increases the impact of using too much or little when using Artelia’s EF. For Cowi’s
EF there is no data for using too much insulation, but using less sees a decrease in the impact
for the lower TC insulation types. A general trend shown, is that using too little results in a
higher impact, at around 6­9% for TC 0.037, compared to using too much, at around 4­5% for
the same insulation. This difference is larger when the TC is lower.

The found optimal thicknesses are generally one step lower in the later time periods. Using

Figure 5.8: Increase in cost from missing the optimum insulation thickness for MF exterior wall
The increase in cost is most severe when using too little insulation, reaching 140% at its most
extreme, than when using too much. Furthermore the increase is highest when using a shorter
calculation period, with the highest difference coinciding with when the optimal thickness is lower
than previous time periods.
Comparison
Overall optimal: The found optimal insulation thicknesses from an economic perspective are
quite similar for both buildings at around 150mm, assuming a TC of 0.037. This is also the
cheapest option, with 0.032 being the second cheapest and 0.034 being the most expensive.
This is different from the environmental analysis, where a lower TC led to higher total emissions.
The cause of this is likely how close in cost the two insulation types are, leading to the smaller
amount needed having an impact over the operational cost. Using a shorter calculation period
also leads to the NPV being lower at all points, including pushing the optimal insulation to one
step thinner in most cases. This makes sense as the operational cost is not calculated for as
long, and as such is lower, while the initial cost is the same. The optimal thicknesses corre­
sponds to an U­value of about 0.224­0.265, which fits decently with the minimum requirements
Environmental and economic optimals
55
==PAGE:64==
5.1. Exterior wall construction
for a new building in BR18, but exceeds the requirements for a renovation.
Variable insulation type: The optimal U­value is pretty similar for the two insulation types with
a TC of 0.034 and 0.032, with 0.037 resulting in a slightly lower optimal U­value. This is rep­
resentative of the higher initial cost for the first two leading to a higher operational cost being
acceptable.
Variable time periods: The optimal insulation thickness is impacted less by the time periods
than by the insulation type used. There is still a decrease in the optimal thickness for the later
time periods, but whether it occurs in 2050 or 2075 varies based on the insulation type.
Impact of missing the optimum: While the impact of using the equivalent of 100mm more or
less insulation than the found optimal looks generally similar for the SF and MF house. There
are however notable exceptions with the impact of using too little insulation in the MF house
2075 having a much higher impact than in the SF. Using too little insulation consistently results
in a higher impact than using too much does. This difference is higher for TC 0.034 and 0.032
insulation, although these two generally have similar impacts from missing. A shorter calculation
period results in higher impacts than a longer one, although the year 2075 is an exception to
this.

SF Results
Figure 5.9: LCCA results for SF exterior walls
Environmental and economic optimals

==PAGE:65==
5.1. Exterior wall construction
As shown in Figure 5.9 the LCCA increases rapidly when using Artelia’s EF’s. This increase
does continue exponentially, and as such it was decided to cut off the y­axis at 100DKK/kg CO2−
eq. to be able to see the resulting LCCA for Cowi’s EF as well. The figure further shows that the
LCCA increases at lower insulation thicknesses in the future.
MF Results
Figure 5.10: LCCA results for MF exterior walls
As shown in Figure 5.10 the LCCA also increases rapidly when using Artelia’s EF for the MF
house. As such the y­axis is also cut off at 100DKK/kg CO2−eq. to be able to see the resulting
LCCA for Cowi’s EF. The LCCA otherwise looks similar to the SF house, albeit with slightly lower
LCCA for each step.
Comparison
Both buildings see similar trends for the LCCA. The increase is less significant when using the
EF by Cowi, but it still sees a steady increase. The first layers of additional insulation will result in
a negative cost for both buildings, reaching zero at about 150mm additional TC 0.037 insulation.
Using insulation with a lower TC results in this point being reached with less additional insulation.
When using Artelia’s EF this point is the same, as it is dependent on the cost, but the increase in
LCCA is much more pronounced. Under these conditions the LCCA quickly shows an exponen­
tial increase resulting in, especially for insulation with a TC of 0.032, a point where the plotted
LCCA is nearly vertical. Additional insulation after, and likely before this point, will therefore be
significantly more expensive than other policies.
Environmental and economic optimals
57
==PAGE:66==
5.2. Roof construction
This point is reached slightly earlier for the SF house, although the difference is at most one step
insulation thickness. Another main takeaway is the variation caused by the time periods. Later
time periods, especially 2075, see a more rapid increase in LCCA at all points. This also includes
when the LCCA reaches zero, which happens with less additional insulation. When this point
is reached does not appear to have any direct correlation with the economic or environmental
optimum as it is between these two thicknesses for all variations.
As such the LCCA for the optimal environmental insulation thickness for the SF house is lower
than the MF house. This is mainly due to this optimal being 25mm lower, at 300mm. The LCCA
for the optimal thickness, of TC 0.037 insulation in the SF house is 4.35DKK/kg CO2 −eq.
For the MF house the optimal thickness under the same conditions is 5.80DKK/kg CO2 −eq.,
when using Artelia’s EF. There are no results of this type for Cowi’s EF, as it does not reach an
optimum.

Comparison
Overall optimal: When using the EF by Cowi all optimums are OoB and as such fall under the
category of ”the more the better”. With the lower EF from Artelia however, the optimal thickness
is well within the range at 350mm insulation with a TC of 0.037 for the MF building and 325mm
for the SF house. Insulations with a lower TC results in higher total GHGe at all thicknesses in
both buildings.
Variable insulation type: While the total GHGe is higher for the higher TC insulation types the
optimal thickness is generally one step lower. There are a few exceptions to this but the optimal
insulation thickness is always lower. It is generally also seen that the MF house has slightly
higher optimal insulation thicknesses than the SF house. It is not a major difference but it is of
interest as it may be caused by the different floor to roof area in the two building types.
Variable time periods: The optimal insulation thicknesses is unchanged from 2025 to 2050, but
falls one step in all but one example, which is in the time period 2075.
Impact of missing the optimum: The impact of using 100mm too little or much insulation is
consistently lower for the MF house. Using the EF from Artelia and insulation with a TC of
0.037 the increase in emissions caused by using too little insulation is 6­8%, depending on the
year. The same value for the SF case results in a 10% increase in emissions. This difference
is pretty consistent when using too little insulation, with the TC 0.032 seeing an even larger
decrease using the same EF. This trend continues when using Cowi’s EF, and is likely caused
by the consistently slightly higher optimal thickness in the MF house.

5.2. Roof construction
Figure 5.15: SF Roof NPV with a period of 50 (Left) and 30 (Right) years.
The NPV of the SF roof depends largely on which type of insulation is used with lower TC’s
resulting in a higher cost. Later time periods do show a minor decrease in cost across the board,
but it is marginal for both the calculation periods. The optimal thicknesses for all insulation types,
time periods and EF’s are shown in Table 5.8.

5.2. Roof construction

too little insulation results in relatively minor increases in NPV of 20­40% for both calculation

insulation with a lower TC the impact is higher in both directions, with a shorter calculation
period resulting in a higher increase. The sole exception to this is insulation with a TC 0.032,
and a calculation period of 50 years, in the SF house which has a much higher change in 2075.
This coincides with the optimal thickness being lower. In fact it puts the impact close to its
counterpart using a calculation period of 30 years. The optimal thickness for these 2 calculations
is the same, further reinforcing that this impact is largely dependant on the thickness.

insulation thickness it appears that this occurs earlier. Comparing the two building components
it is true that the LCCA is higher for the same insulation thickness.
For the SF house, using Artelia’s EF, the current environmental optimum is 325mm, with a
resulting LCCA of 11.90DKK/kg CO2 −eq. Using the same assumptions the optimum for the
MF house is 300mm with a resulting LCCA of 5.24DKK/kg CO2 −eq.
Another trend that continues is that the LCCA increases in the future. The increase in cost
for the same insulation thickness is particularly large between 2050 and 2075, as the heating
requirements are decreased due to the higher mean temperature, while the cost is kept stable

Comparison
Overall optimal: When Cowi’s EF is used, the overall least emitting option for the SF house is
103mm of TC 0.031 EPS insulation. The alternative EF from Artelia results in a too low optimal

significant difference between the buildings optimal thicknesses is likely caused by the different
strength requirements for the buildings. As shown in Table 4.21 the difference in embodied
emissions for the two types is significant when needing a compressive strength of 80kN, but

5.3. Terrain deck construction
increase is much lower. The low optimal thickness further reinforce this, as it insinuates that
the operational emissions are lesser compared to the embodied.
Variable insulation type: The trends between the two types of EPS used differs for the buildings.
In the MF house using the EPS with a lower TC decreases the optimal thickness. For the SF
house this change actually results in an increased optimal thickness, which has not been seen
in any of the other results. The increase in thickness is minor due to the differences in thickness
per step, and are as such seen better on the optimal U­values. This is likely caused by the
embodied emissions for the two types being very similar for the SF house, as discussed in the
overall optimal.
Most of the variations between the two types of EPS can be seen in the analysis of the overall
optimal, but the trends differ in the two buildings. For the MF house using a lower optimal
thickness in all cases, while the SF house sees an increase in optimal thickness. It is minor due
to the differences in the insulation thickness steps, and can therefore be seen more clearly in
the lower optimal U­value.
Variable time periods: There is a minor variation in the optimal depending on the year for both
buildings. The SF house sees a consistent one step down in 2075, using Cowi’s EF, while the
differences in the MF house vary depending on the EF and insulation type used.
Impact of missing the optimum: The impact of using the equivalent to 100mm insulation too
much or little depends greatly which building is being investigated. For the SF house there is
only data for too little for the TC 0.031 Cowi EF, resulting in a 20% increase for 2025 and 2050.
There is not data for 2075. If too much insulation is used the impact varies greatly depending
on the EF, with Cowi’s resulting in a 10% increase and Artelia’s 70­105% increase. This large
variety is dependant on the year.
The MF house sees a greater increase when using too little at 30­75%, depending on the year
and insulation type. Using too much with Cowi’s EF results in a similar 10% increase, but
Artelia’s only results in 30­55% increase. This is significantly lower tjan for the SF house.

Comparison
Overall optimal: Looking at the difference in NPV the two insulation types result in a very even
cost for both the buildings. The type of insulation is therefore secondary with the optimal U­
value equivalent for the SF house being 0.208, either 75mm TC 0.038 or 62mm TC 0.031 EPS
insulation. For the MF house the current optimal U­value is 0.147, either 150mm TC 0.038 or
124mm TC 0.031 EPS insulation. If a shorter calculation period is used these optimal thick­
nesses each decrease by one step.
These optimal thicknesses are decently close to the minimum U­values for new buildings in
BR18, with especially the 0.208 being close to the limit of 0.20. When comparing to the re­
quired values from §279, which sets the maximum U­value for the terrain deck at 0.10, the
found optimums do not comply with this regulation.
Variable insulation type: The optimal thickness is the same for both types of EPS insulation
used.
Variable time periods: The optimal insulation thicknesses do decrease in later time periods
with all variations optimum being one step lower in 2075. An exception to this is the SF house
when using a calculation period of 30 years, as this results in the insulation thickness being the
thinnest simulated and therefore OoB.
Impact of missing the optimum: The impact of using the equivalent of 100mm insulation too
much or little varies based on the building and calculation period. For the SF house data for
using too little is limited due to the low optimal thickness. Using too much however, results in
an increase of 10­32% with the higher end mostly being from a calculation period of 30 years.
In the MF house using too much results in a 10­20% increase, while using too little results in a
30­60% increase. This follows the found trend that using too little insulation increases the NPV
by more than using too much, although these values are on the lower end when compared to
missing with a similar amount in the other building parts.

Environmental

The resulting GHGe’s for the simulated window to floor ratio’s can be seen in Figure 5.31.
Figure 5.31: SF Window emissions using EF from Cowi (Left) and Artelia (Right)
These results show that a higher window to floor area results in higher GHGe, regardless of the

==PAGE:84==
5.4. Window to floor ratio
Figure 5.32: MF Window emissions using EF from Cowi (Left) and Artelia (Right)
The trends for the MF house are very similar to the SF house, with lower ratios resulting in lower

Comparison
The Figures showing the environmental emissions for various window to floor ratios show very
similar results for both the SF and MF house. Having a higher window to floor ratio always
results in a higher GHGe, regardless of the window type and year. This is not surprising as the
windows serve multiple purposes, such as allowing occupants to see outside the building, let
light in and cool the building, which has not been taken into consideration for this thesis. Of
more interest is the difference between using the EF calculated by Cowi or Artelia.
When using the higher EF from Cowi a 3 pane window results in significantly lower GHGe for
all time periods and ratios. This fits well with the window requirements from BR18 §258, which
indirectly states that windows must be Energy label A. When using the EF from Artelia the
results look very different though. In the first time periods a 2 pane window results in lower total
GHGe for all ratios. Moving into the future the two window types results in closer and closer
total emissions, being almost the same values by 2075.
As such, while larger window areas always results in higher emissions, the better window type
depend solely on the DH EF.

==PAGE:86==
6
Discussion
The main goal of this thesis was investigating the environmentally optimal insulation thicknesses
in the Danish building envelope, now and in the future. In this chapter the key findings from this
will be presented, along with the limitations and uncertainties that were found during the project,
and what future work can be inspired by the findings here. A further aim was to produce these
results in a way that allows future policy makers to compare the feasibility of policies regarding
heat losses in buildings against other technologies.
6.1
Key findings
One of the main findings in this thesis is the optimal insulation thicknesses found for both a
SF and MF house. These optimal thicknesses vary based on the EF of the DH supplying the
buildings with heat, what the TC of the insulation is and the time period that is being simulated.
Probably the most impactful finding was how big of an impact the EF of the DH has on the
optimal thicknesses. These findings fit existing literature that was found and presented during
the literature review of this thesis. Here it was stated that EF’s above 25 −30g CO2 −eq.
resulted in optimal thicknesses above 500mm. In this thesis two different EF’s were used, one
by Cowi and one by Artelia. Cowi’s EF ranged from 68.04 −70.45g CO2 −eq., for the different
time periods, and is as such above the stated limit. The alternative EF by Artelia ranged from
13.20 −15.39g CO2 −eq., which is well below the limit.
Using the higher EF by Cowi resulted in the optimal thicknesses exceeding 500mm of TC 0.037
insulation, for the exterior walls and the roof. When using the lower EF by Artelia these optimal
insulation thicknesses, using the same assumptions, were found the be in the range of 300 −
350mm.
The terrain deck differed from these results, and also showed significant differences between
the two buildings, likely caused by the different requirements set for the insulation here. For the
MF house both EF’s found an optimal insulation thickness, albeit with a significant difference
between them, while the lower EF resulted in too low thicknesses to be considered valid. As
such optimal insulation thicknesses have been found for all building parts, although they are
highly dependant on the future projections of the heating grid.
Looking at one of the most common holes in the building envelope, windows, the different EF’s
also had a large impact. While a higher window to floor ratio was never found to result in a
lower total GHGe, there was a difference in the optimal amount panes in the window. Assuming
a lower EF 2 pane windows resulted in less emissions for both buildings, while a higher EF
resulted in 3 pane windows being less emitting. Economically the two window types resulted in
very similar NPV for all ratios.
6.1.1
Variations between the building types
The found optimal thicknesses for each building part are quite similar for the two buildings.
Generally speaking it has been found that the MF house has a slightly higher optimum, with
about 25mm more TC 0.037 insulation. This is true for all investigated time periods for the roof.
The optimal thickness in the exterior wall instead converge for the buildings, resulting in the
same optimal thicknesses from 2050 and onwards for both buildings.
The optimal thicknesses for the terrain deck vary significantly between the two buildings, likely
caused by the differences in the EPS insulation used.
 Environmental and economic optimals
78
==PAGE:87==
6.1. Key findings
6.1.2
Variations in insulation types
A trend found when comparing the GHGe of the different mineral wool types was that the mineral
wool with a TC of 0.037, the highest investigated, always resulted in the lowest total emissions
and cost. The single outlier to this was the NPV for the exterior walls, where the insulation with
a TC of 0.032 was the second cheapest. This is also the only example where the material cost
was very close to the nearest one. As such from an economic perspective insulation with a
lower TC is not worth the cost, unless the cost per cubic meter is similar. It is not possible to
know how close this price would have to be from the results in this thesis.
The same is true when referring to GHGe, where the option with the lowest embodied emissions
almost always resulted in the lowest total GHGe. This is particularly noticeable on the results
using a low EF for heating, where the differences in total GHGe became apparent. Using a
higher EF, such as the one from Cowi, the tendency is still in effect, but the curves are closer to
each other, making a potential trade off worth it. This however has not been the case in these
simulations.
If reducing the thickness of the building part is of high importance then using insulation with a
lower TC might be worth it, but it depends heavily on how much thickness needs to be saved.
That said, according to the findings in this thesis it is environmentally better to use too much
insulation rather than too little. While it does depend on the calculation period, using 100mm
insulation more than the optimum generally resulted in an increase in emissions of about 5% for
insulation with a TC of 0.037. When using insulation with a lower TC, and therefore embodied
emissions, this saw a slight increase. Using the same amount of insulation too little varied a
lot more, especially depending on the calculation period, but was consistently higher. From an
economic context the same tendency is shown, with a lot higher increases.
6.1.3
Economic and environmental optimum
When comparing the economic and environmental optimums a clear trend emerges. In almost
all cases the economic optimum is significantly lower than the environmental one, often being
only half the thickness. The main driver of this is the high cost of insulation when compared to
the cost of DH.
By including the NPV as the LCC of the component it was possible to calculate LCCA values for
all the building parts. These have shown that by limiting the heat loss, further than the minimum
requirements set in BR18, often leads to both an economic and environmental benefit. This
economic benefit is always the case for the exterior wall, where the LCCA is negative at lower
values for all TC’s. This is not true for all building parts, with the roof only having a negative
LCCA for the TC 0.037 insulation. This value further increases when more insulation is added.
The LCCA is not necessarily negative for the terrain deck either, but in this case it depends on
whether it is for the SF or the MF house. Due to the low optimum for the SF terrain deck the
LCCA values that were found increased rapidly. While this is eventually true for all the insulation
types, it is especially visible in the terrain deck. The MF terrain deck LCCA is negative for most
of the time, before exponentially increasing. This eventual rapid increase is seen in all the
building parts, although not at the same thicknesses and rate. Generally the later time periods,
2050 and especially 2075, see these earlier and with faster increases. This is likely caused
by the expected lower need for heating which decreases the need for thicker insulation. This
is further reinforced by the insulation types with a lower TC also seeing this trend at lower
additional insulation over the baseline. This leads to the conclusion that it is absolutely possible
to minimise the cost of conserving carbon through proper insulation of buildings. It is however
necessary to not insulate too much, as the LCCA see very fast increases in cost once this line
is crossed. And where this line is depends on the time period, building part and insulation type.
Environmental and economic optimals
79
==PAGE:88==
6.2. Limitations and uncertainties
6.2
Limitations and uncertainties
6.2.1
Material prices
A major uncertainty in this thesis is the material prices. These prices are aimed at the private
population, and as such are not applicable to the building industry at whole, as prices here are
significantly lower. Unfortunately it was not possible to obtain a comprehensive list of insulation
prices from the industry, as this type of data is kept confidential. It was however possible to
obtain a small sample of Stark’s insulation prices at a corporate scale. From this the price of
flexible insulation with a TC of 0.037 was set at a price of about 335DKK/m3, while 0.034 was
about 435DKK/m3. These prices are significantly lower, and closer to each other, than the
ones found on Stark’s website. This leads to a significant difference in the economic analysis,
and therefore likely a higher optimal insulation thickness. These results are therefore likely
different when larger companies are in charge of construction.
Another uncertainty regarding this is whether the costs will stay stable throughout the years.
Potential large scale changes to the production, e.g. electrification, will be costly for the produc­
ers. This could therefore lead to increases in material prices. A similar trend could be possible
for DH and electricity, where major changes to the grid would need to be funded.
6.2.2
Insulation types
The investigation in this thesis is limited to mineral wool insulation in the roof and the exterior
walls, and roof, and EPS insulation in the terrain deck. While these are common insulation
materials in Denmark they are not the only ones. Using more environmentally friendly insulation
materials, such as wood or paper based insulation, might result in significantly different optimal
thicknesses. A further limitation is that the generic data used is for mineral wool, which could be
split into glass and stone wool. As these materials would result in different embodied emissions
this could also lead to different optimal thicknesses.
6.2.3
Future material emissions
The background data for the changes in embodied emissions for the various materials are not
known. It is known that they are based on the PREMISE program, which does not include large
scale changes to the production line. As such changes such as large scale electrification is not
included despite this potentially reducing emissions by a large degree. In case of these kinds
of changes a new calculation would be necessary.
6.3
Future work
6.3.1
Different walls constructions
This project has only used a wall construction using concrete, insulation and bricks. While this is
a common construction in Denmark it is not the only one. As such making similar optimisations
for other construction methods, e.g. a timber frame structure, and seeing whether the results
are similar would be very relevant. It would further be relevant to see whether the found optimal
thicknesses are similar for other types of buildings, e.g. offices and schools.
6.3.2
Future legislation
Results like the ones found in this thesis are a potential basis for future building legislation. It has
been shown that the optimal U­values for each part does not correspond to the requirements set
in BR18. When future building regulations are made taking results such as these into account
would be very relevant.
Another relevant part is the inclusion of LCCA values. These allows better comparison between
different policies. As such they could aid i deciding whether emissions should be saved from
the heating of buildings or alternative policies, ensuring the most efficient use of capital.
Environmental and economic optimals
80
==PAGE:89==
7
Conclusion
The aim of this thesis was to find and investigate the optimal insulation thicknesses in the build­
ing envelope of two different Danish houses. A SF and a MF house. This has been done for
a contemporary period, a medium­term future and a long­term future using dynamic building
simulations with variable insulation thicknesses. It has been found that the optimal insulation
thicknesses depend mostly on three factors. The embodied emissions, the operational emis­
sions, and the weather.
The operational emissions: have been limited to heating the buildings, using two different EF’s
for DH. The official EF’s from BR18, calculated and updated by Cowi in 2020, and EF’s calcu­
lated by Artelia in 2023.
The embodied emissions: have been included using generic data for Denmark. This generic
data has further been updated to account for all valid EPD’s on the market at the time of writ­
ing. To account for expected changes and reduction in these emissions a projection has been
applied, reducing the materials embodied emissions for each future.
Changes to the climate: have been included through three different weather files for different
time periods. The used files include projections of the worst case scenario climate change.
Using these assumptions it has been found that the optimal insulation thicknesses depend
mostly on the EF of the DH with the lower EF by Artelia resulting in optimal thicknesses ranging
from 300 to 350mm mineral wool insulation with a TC of 0.037 for the exterior walls and the
roof of both the SF and MF house. The expected climate change has not been found to have a
major impact on these thicknesses, although minor decreases have been found. Investigating
mineral wool with a lower TC have been found to not result in lower total GHGe at any thick­
ness, nor resulting in a lower NPV. When assuming a higher EF for the district heating these
findings look very different though, with the optimal insulation thickness exceeding 500mm for
both buildings. It can therefore not be said that there is a definite optimum for the insulation
thickness until more knowledge on the DH grid is known.
The economic optimal insulation thickness has been found to be significantly lower than the
environmental thicknesses, ranging from 150­200mm for the exterior wall and roof. The main
driver of this has been found to be the high initial cost from purchasing mineral wool, compared
to the lower cost of DH.
By combining the GHGe and NPV a LCCA value has been calculated based on a baseline of
the minimum requirements from BR18. This has resulted in a figure for each building part and
three mineral wool types being produced, showing that using more insulation than the minimum
will both conserve carbon and be economically beneficial. The LCCA does see an exponential
increase, especially when using a low EF for the DH. This increase is further found to happen
at lower insulation thicknesses in the future.
Regarding the use of 3 pane windows the simulations have shown that this depends on the
EF of DH. Simulations using Artelia’s found that using 3 pane windows resulted in higher total
emissions and cost when compared to a 2 pane window. Cowi’s EF found the opposite with 3
pane windows being both cheaper and lower emitting. As such it is not possible to conclude
anything here until more knowledge of the actual EF is known.
 Environmental and economic optimals
